B001R4MEYS|5.0|I don't know how Mastodon do it, but once again they've managed to release an album that tops their previous effort as career highlight; Mastodon just keep getting better and better. Crack The Skye takes the ball 'Blood Mountain,' brought and runs with it, The music is even more progressive, even more virtuosic and the material is even more dense.<br /><br />Somehow Mastodon have also simultaneously reinvented themselves and stayed true to their trademark sound at the same time. The jangly, awkward riffs are the same and the complex jazzy drumming is the same, but the music has a fresh, cleaner and at first glance more 'listenable,' air to it; in addition to this the production is amazing, the artwork is in keeping with the tone of all Mastodon artwork to date and once again the album is a concept album. No doubt a few fans in Remission T-shirts will still label this album as a sell out despite the fact that this is Mastodon's least commercial offering to date due to the length, density and originality of the music.<br /><br />Fans of the direction Mastodon started to take with Leviathan and especially with Blood Mountain will welcome Crack The Skye as the next logical step; praising the numerous guitar solos, haunting clean vocals and entertaining lyrics.  Everything you'd expect in terms of passion and performance is represented here and represented in stronger doses than ever before. The album is bursting with new ideas, with exciting bass lines, complicated time shifts and yet all the force you'd expect from the band who wrote Remission.<br /><br />If you have an open mind there is a hell of a lot to enjoy here, such as the new Keys and synths, along with longer songs and cleaner vocals (plus more vocalists) that add whole new dimensions to the sound that Mastodon previously only hinted at in songs like 'Ol Nessie,' and 'Sleeping Giant.'<br /><br />Some may be surprised to find that in addition to the amazing Troy and the aggressive Brent, Brann Dailor appears to be lending his vocal talents to the album. Additionally, many fans will welcome Scott Kelly back for yet another wonderful guest vocal appearance.<br /><br />There is little point in naming standout tracks as the entire album is the best thing the band have ever done, but if I was forced to pick a favorite  I'd pick 'The Czar,' which is probably the coolest song you'll hear all year, that or 'The Last Baron,' which has some beautiful acoustic moments, some lead epic guitar moments and an awesome section that seems like a cheeky tribute to '21st Century Schizoid Man,' over all the song is like a bizarre cross between 'Trampled Under Hoof,' with 'This Mortal Soil,' and the drumming is just so impressive.<br /><br />Overall; Although at first the album may seem like a departure, repeat listens will reveal moments that could fit at the end of 'Hearts Alive,' or fit into the middle of 'Mother Puncher.' In fact the first single 'Divinations,' sounds like it could fit well on the Blood Mountain album. B004M9ZI0M|5.0|William Shakespeare's THE TEMPEST is probably his last play, written in 1610-11, and as such it has some of the more eloquent passages of soliloquies of any of his works. In the original version the story is set on a remote island, 'where Prospero, the exiled Duke of Milan, plots to restore his daughter Miranda to her rightful place, using illusion and skilful manipulation. The eponymous tempest brings to the island Prospero's usurping brother Antonio and the complicit Alonso, King of Naples. There, his machinations bring about the revelation of Antonio's low nature, the redemption of Alonso, and the marriage of Miranda to Alonso's son, Ferdinand.' Enter Julie Taymor and the imaginative play becomes even more so with her deft re-writing and direction and use of visual effects.  In Taymor's versions 'the main character is now a woman named Prospera. Going back to the 16th or 17th century, women practicing the magical arts of alchemy were often convicted of witchcraft. In Taymor's version, Prospera is usurped by her brother and sent off with her four-year daughter on a ship. She ends up on an island; it's a tabula rasa: no society, so the mother figure becomes a father figure to Miranda. This leads to the power struggle and balance between Caliban and Prospera; a struggle not about brawn, but about intellect.'<br /><br />Taymor and Shakespeare together make the important character of Ariel, Prospera's obedient sprite, a thing of magic: Ben Wishaw darts and floats and flies about apparently in the buff in a most ingenious fashion, delivering his lines in perfect Shakespearean cadence (his 'Full fathom five thy father lies... ' is exquisite).  The transformation of Prospero to Prospera is magical with Helen Mirren once again proving that she is an incomparably fine actress (one great moment is her delivery of the lines 'Our revels now are ended. These our actors, As I foretold you, were all spirits and are melted into air, into thin air: And, like the baseless fabric of this vision, The cloud-capp'd towers, the gorgeous palaces, The solemn temples, the great globe itself, Ye all which it inherit, shall dissolve And, like this insubstantial pageant faded, Leave not a rack behind. We are such stuff As dreams are made on, and our little life Is rounded with a sleep.')<br /><br />THE TEMPEST is an odd assortment of magic, treachery, young love, silly comedy, and odd goings on, but filled with a cast such as Taymor has selected it jumps alive with passion and glee.  Caliban is Djimon Hounsou, Miranda is Felicity Jones, The King of Naples is David Strathairn and his son Ferdinand is young Reeve Carney, Prospera's brother Antonio is Chris Cooper and his sidekick Sebastian is Alan Cumming, and the two actors assigned to the buffoon roles are Albert Molina and Russell brand.  Gonzalo is Tim Conti.  This  tightened Tempest works well though one wonders how much of the opening scenes' shipwreck (due to Prospera's calling upon the tempest) adds to the overall story.  Yet in Taymor's vision it all comes together beautifully.  The sung portions of the play and the musical sore in general are from the intelligent pen of Elliot Goldenthal.  Recommended! 1400032717|4.0|I'm reading many novels, but I find myself being absorbed by novels where people need to overcome obstacles within themselves in order to resolve or come to terms with the obstacles surrounding their physical life. For example, I started The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Night-time and finished it today. It's an amazing tale about a high functioning autistic savant whose parents are behaving foolishly. Christopher is the narrator and his problems with living are astronomically complicated, but his parents are also suffering through difficulties which while not on the same scale as their son are also complicated. All three have to struggle with their own natures to actually solve anything, and the learning to quell their inner voices for better functioning does not mean they get what they wanted. It's an unfair compromise with Reality, and Reality imposes the final terms and boundaries of their hopes. Even though Christopher is the one diagnosed as 'special needs' it's obvious his parents are struggling with the special needs that come from living with a kid far different from ordinary children. Christopher is high maintenance and there do not seem to be any rewards in parenting such a child. He requires abnormal behavior from everyone around him that must be in a certain regulated and regimated fashion that soothes him enough to not act out. His parents need his affection and love, but he can't tolerate their looking at him or touching him, much less any emotions. The book is Christopher's journal so in a bit the reader soon gets a read on who Christopher is. He is charming in an odd way, he is so helpless but he cannot comprehend the entirety of his deficits or the serious handicapping of his dream to be an astronaut. Although he cannot bear emotions from anyone he is not unemotional. Anyone with empathy feels Christopher's agonies but he cannot be consoled by empathy on any level. His favorite sleeping dream is when he walks an earth where he is the only person alive. This is not a person to love, yet his parents adore him while at the same time tearing their lives to pieces by their unrequited love for him. It is a desperate existence for all of this family, but nothing can be done except to bear it, handling each Autistic meltdown with the only response that works: waiting it out patiently sitting nearby until it's over. I could not do this, raise a child this functionally broken without any reward or affection or gratefulness. Even dogs can like and love back, enjoy petting or want to be with you - none of which is something Christopher can give or do. However, these parents never stop loving their son. It appears the Special Needs Counselor is the one person who helps Christopher understand how to share his life as much as he can, which is little better than understanding how different people are from him and that he must tolerate their presence without instigating or resorting to violence. He learns phrases that are meaningless to him, such as \\"how are you today\\" simply because he has learned these things minimize his contact with people. To not be polite extends the always unwelcome interest of all persons in his vicinity. This book is well-written and imagined, I could not put it down. Christopher's mind is as illuminated as a normal person would want from a good book. The parents are not special people except in their love for their unloving child. They are blue collar, not particularly sophisticated or wealthy. The son baffles them and in their sorrow and disappointment which they cannot verbalize they attempt to soothe themselves with alcohol and sex, but finds themselves once the temporary diversion is over in the same depressing reality. There is no cure, only palliatives. A strong book that enlightens readers about the cruel inescapable cage the handicapped child and his parents must come to endure, finding little help except for whatever resources they can discover within themselves and if lucky, in the community.mmunity.

B0046E825G|1.0|Stephanie Meyer writes vampire fiction as many teenage girls do -- overblown writing, sexy cold vampires, and a vacuously attractive heroine who serves as the readers' stand-in. WARNING: spoilers.<br /><br />But as fluffy and shameless as Meyer's past work has been, she dips far below her past nadirs in the ghastly, sluggish \\"Breaking Dawn.\\" There's a grotesque twist toward the book's spluttering end, and it becomes painfully obvious that Meyer was determined to give everyone an utterly happy ending. And she'll sacrifice whatever plot or characterization it takes to get that happy ending.<br /><br />Resident \\"Mary Sue\\" Bella is getting the royal treatment from her fiance Edward in the days before they marry. The wedding goes off with only one hitch -- her ex-love-interest Jacob's anger at her impending transformation into a vampire. But newlywed bliss doesn't make for a terribly interesting grand finale, so we get the expected twist -- Bella's pregnant with a half-vampire baby.<br /><br />Jacob becomes even more upset when he witnesses the violent birth of Bella's magical super-special baby Renesmee (nickname: Nessie) -- only to have something unexpected (and very icky) occur between himself and the newborn. But turning into a vampire won't solve Bella's problems, because it turns out that other vampires are gathering in preparation to attack... and it's due to the birth of Renesmee.<br /><br />Authors often create all sorts of endings for popular series -- sad ones, happy ones, bittersweet ones, and occasionally ones that make absolutely no sense in relation to the series. Unfortunately in \\"Breaking Dawn,\\" Meyer has opted for the last type of ending -- and that finale starts the moment Bella gets pregnant, and becomes more absurd and inane as the story drags on. It ends up being rather difficult to care what happens to any of the characters, because the readers are being assaulted by a string of absurd plot twists.<br /><br />Not that there's much of a plot to twist -- Meyer desperately pads out the flimsy storyline with a lot of florid prose and endless melodrama. The first part of the book is pretty much consumed by wedding drivel and subsequent soft-focus sex, but after Bella becomes pregnant everything spirals down the drain. All this drama culminates in a ghastly birth scene that may turn some readers off ever having kids. Vampire teeth are involved.<br /><br />Having exhausted what little plot can be wrung from a Edward-Bella marriage and subsequent pregnancy, Meyer struggles to somehow fix this by introducing a Big Bad Threat to the new family's happiness. Unfortunately, it has all the climactic impact of a soggy firecracker. As the final indignity, Meyer crashes down a vaguely pedophilic plot twist for Jacob, so he won't be too lonely. You can almost hear the thin plot cracking in a thousand places, as Meyers strains to give a Happily-Ever-After for everybody.<br /><br />Bella remains one of the most vacuous heroines in modern literature -- she has the passion and intellect of a wet mop, but once she becomes a vampire she is also shockingly graceful, beautiful, powerful and self-controlled. In case you haven't noticed, she's just amazing in every way -- and it's difficult to control one's gag reflex when reading about a glaring self-insert made so utterly perfect.<br /><br />Not that the supporting characters help much -- Edward has yet to develop an actual personality beyond adoring Bella, and poor Jacob is treated like a maniac. And the Ultra-Super-Special Vampire Baby is almost as nauseating -- and even more featureless a personality -- as the mother who spawned her.<br /><br />\\"Breaking Dawn\\" would better have been called \\"Breaking Down.\\" Stephanie Meyer had a good thing going with the vacuous vampire soap opera, but here she rips it apart and turns it inside out. Intensely bad.flimsy storyline with a lot of florid prose and endless melodrama. 0393064476|1.0|For the past 40 years Prof. Stephen Greenblatt has been an influential scholar of Renaissance literature. He truly does know his stuff. However, his influence has not always been seen as beneficial because of an unfortunate tendency towards questionable interpretation. Too often, his books resemble very tall, very unstable houses of cards - impressive, but liable to collapse at the slightest breath of critical analysis.<br /><br />Greenblatt's Hot New Book is The Swerve: How the World Became Modern. \\"Modern,\\" in his view, means \\"Non-Religious\\" and the swerve came when a 15th century Italian scholar, on a book-hunting tour through old German monasteries, stumbled across a copy of On the Nature of Things, a philosophic Latin poem written by Lucretius shortly before the birth of Christ. With the rediscovery of this one ancient manuscript, Greenblatt believes, the Western mind was able to liberate itself from religious dogma, rediscover the joy of living for this life (rather than the After-life), and create the Modern World we know today.<br /><br />This narrative is more than a little over-the-top. It is also simply wrong, on two fundamental counts<br /><br />Greenblatt believes that Lucretius' philosophy had been a dominant school of thought in the ancient world, but was stifled in the early centuries AD because it contradicted the doctrine of the Christian Church. He also believes that this same philosophy - rediscovered in the early 15th century - was the great Game-Changer in Renaissance thought. Neither belief is true.<br /><br />Any competent historian of ancient philosophy knows that, far from being a dominant force, Epicureanism was actually a minor player in the world of Greco-Roman thought - much less popular and influential than its principle rivals, Stoicism and Platonism. The philosophy of Lucretius failed to catch on not because it was un-Christian, but because pagan Greeks and Romans found its dogma of the Randomness of the Universe so intellectually and spiritually unsatisfying. Stoicism and Platonism were very different, but both schools were convinced that we live in a Cosmos, not a Chaos. Both taught that our world is not an infinite collection of atoms bouncing around randomly and forming temporary - and meaningless - conglomerates of Being. It is the product of Providential Wisdom.<br /><br />The Epicureanism of Lucretius was no more dominant after the Christian Middle Ages than it had been before. Throughout the 15th, 16th and early-17th centuries, Platonism and Stoicism (especially the former) were once again the schools of thought most enthusiastically revived, and most influential. To contradict just one example in Greenblatt's book, Botticelli's art was philosophically informed by the Renaissance Neoplatonism of Marsilio Ficino, not by Lucretius. In the early modern world as in pagan antiquity, Epicureanism occupied a position rather like today's American Libertarian Party - a fringe-movement with a few passionate devotees but no real clout.<br /><br />Many scholars questioned Greenblatt's insights into Shakespeare (as found in his 2005 \\"speculative biography\\" of the Bard, Will in the World). I believe even more doubt will be heaped upon this very questionable reading of early modern philosophy.cism and Platonism were very different, but both schools were convinced that we live in a Cosmos, not a Chaos. Both taught that our world is not an infinite collection of atoms bouncing around randomly and forming temporary - and meaningless - conglomerates of Being. 1933633867|2.0|Prof. Graeber is obviously an extremely competent anthropologist, and this well referenced book (with an over forty page bibliography!) contains some very plausible propositions.  One is that credit systems emerged before barter and money, and that the state or other authorities with a monopoly on violence instated currency from the top down. Another is that our modern fiat money system is built on nothing more than trust, unreflective of any fundamentals whatsoever (although this is obvious to anyone who has studied our modern financial system, and is noted in many of the textbooks Prof. Graeber likes to slight). Unfortunately,  apart from these two ideas, this book is disorganized, sloppily written and lacks an overarching thesis to tie its chapters together. One theme Prof. Graeber seeks to return to again and again is that our language of morality is shrouded in words we also use to describe financial transactions, but this rather simple observation is drowned in anthropological anecdotes and a rather long winded explication of Bruno Tharet's bizzare \\"primordial debt theory\\", both of which tend to obfuscate rather than clarify the matter at hand. His investigation of different modes of economic relations, which he titles communistic, exchange, and hierarchical strikes me as clumsily composed and derivative-there have been many challenges to the rational actor model of economic decision making (by Amartya Sen, Robert Frank etc) that Graeber both fails to mention and which are much more illuminating to the interested reader. His final chapter is particularly odd. He urges us to feel sympathy for the non-industrious poor  because they \\"aren't doing us any harm\\"  He attempts to refute the assertion  of Austrian economist Von Mises (who I am not fan of) that in our current economy all creditors are not evil landlords but often ordinary individuals with a sneer rather than an argument. He encourages us to think of ways to reorganize our social and economic relations so that we are not so reliant upon debt, but does not offer any ideas in this vein (although he has challenged his responsibility to do so). There are many problems in the world today, from poverty in \\"third world countries\\" to lack of healthcare in the US. Rather than muse on the genealogy of morals and yearn for a return to the relations of extant primitive tribes, I'd prefer to get down to the hard business of figuring out pragmatic solutions to relieve suffering and improve our quality of life. While this book does offer some very creative thinking, I am not sure I would recommend it.

B005HG4C4C|3.0|I can't bring myself to like it or dislike it. It's just an album, nothing special. I was really hoping that with the departure of Portnoy micromanagement that the remaining four members would be free to create a really stellar album. Instead, it's the same formula as Train of Thought and Octavarium; good bye Progressive Rock, hello Contemporary Christian. I don't have an issue with the musical direction it seems that DT has taken. I do take issue with the uninspired and bland content of the album.  ____    Petrucci's guitar are blaring the whole time and drown everything else out. Some of his timings and meter are crazy complicated and are cool to listen too. It just seems that, if LaBrie didn't sing at all, I would have guess that this was Petrucci's second solo album.    LaBrie vocals are good. He's holding his own as best as anyone with a vocal cord injury and hitting high notes for the past 20 some-odd years can do. Can he still hit the high notes from Images and Words and Awake? Certainly not, but he still has solid control over his voice and recognizes what he is capable of.     You get a hint of Myung, who really deserves a bigger part in the grand scheme of things. He's an incredible musician and think he has been forced into the back seat long enough.    Rudess is more subdued in the album, and at least (like with Systematic Chaos and Black Clouds and Silver Linings), he doesn't completely ruin a song with his disharmonious and random \\"squibbles\\" and \\"boops\\" he seems so fond of.    Mangini totally got shafted. All you hear from him is a bit of snare, bland kick drums, a slew of cymbals and no fills on the toms. After reading that the songs were already written, and they basically said \\"here's your drum tracks. Play this, and you can embellish what you like.\\" That's like with Henry Ford and the Model T, \\"You can have it in any color, so long as it's black.\\" There's no opportunity for Mangini to add his personal creativity or personality to the album. And something new and fresh is what DT needs right now. I'm disappointed that they didn't capitalize on the addition of Mangini.  ______    On the Backs of Angels - The song is alright. I listened to the prerelease on youtube from a link on their website and wasn't particularly impressed nor did I just outright dislike it. It has a good lead in, and builds the progression, but there really isn't a climax to it. It's an unremarkable song. Not good, not bad.    Build me up, break me down - Probably my favorite track of the album. Has some great, heavy riffs and the chorus flows well with the rest of the verse.    Lost not forgotten - Some nice keywork by Rudess at the beginning. I really like the verse on this track, but the chorus is so contrasting in rhythm it breaks the harmony of the rest of the song. This track reminds me of so many others on DT last few albums. I really like parts of the song, and dislike other parts of it. Rarely liking the whole song.    This is the life - A decent ballad actually. Some of the word choices don't flow well when LaBrie holds them, however. Rudess, Petrucci and Mangini work well together on this track....where's Myung?    Bridges in the sky - This track sounds like they took samples from seven different songs from four different albums and spliced them together. It's a hodgepodge of different harmonies and rhythms and one cluttered mess of a song.  Outcry - This one is similar to Bridges in the Sky. There is so much going on and so much varying of the harmonies that it sounds like a big mishmash of sound ....the last two and a half minutes of the track are decent though.    Far from heaven - Another ballade with Rudess and LaBrie performing a duet. It's more of a filler track on the album like On the Backs of Angels. Nothing special about it.    Breaking all Illusions - Another rollercoaster with bits of speed and heavy riffs mixed with slow moments all mingling together. I can remember the days of Learning to Live, A Change of Seasonsand This Dying Soul when the bridges and change-ups all worked together and made for one complete song. Can't find that on this one.    Beneath the Surface - The last track and ballade on the album. Not a bad song, not a great one. Like many other of the tracks on the album, there's nothing about it that makes me want to listen to it over and over.   ____    I've listened to A Dramatic Turn of Events several times, and as much as I want to, I can't get any sort of arousal out of it. I didn't hold the album up to a whole lot of expectations; I wasn't looking for an album as bone jarring as Awake, or explorative as Falling into Infinity or even as simple as Train of Thought. I wanted to see Dream Theater get out of the rut that has been their last few albums, and with A Dramatic Turn of Events, it ends up being lack luster and ultimately boring. This is just another album to take up another 1/4inch on my shelf and collect dust.    From all the readings on different websites and even looking at the composer and writer of the lyrics from previous albums and seeing Portnoy's name on everything, I figured without him, DT would have taken the opportunity and reinvent themselves. Upon hearing the album, nothing has changed. Maybe Portnoy's \\"micromanagement\\" wasn't really the problem.

0316017930|3.0|Outliers is a very interesting book. The idea is that success comes from doing many small things better rather than doing/having just one big thing better, like talent or IQ. Yes, talent and IQ are parts of the equation (Gladwell says that you only need a certain amount of each and then you get diminishing returns), but other things come into play too. Also important are hard work (Gladwell says that 10,000 hours is the threshold), timing (being born at the right time for the right industries helps), culture (Gladwell says that Asians may be better at math because of ancient cultural rice farming techniques), and luck, among others. Compelling examples include the Beatles, Bill Gates, Mozart, and 19th century Industrialists. All came along at the right time for their particular industries, had \\"enough\\" talent (although they were not necessarily the most gifted in this area), worked very hard, were in the right place at the right time (Gates had almost limitless access to very expensive computers in 1968), and were downright lucky.<br /><br />Although the stories and explanations in the book are fascinating, as I was reading I began to wonder what would have happened to these people if one or more of Gladwell's conditions were missing. Would the Beatles have simply been a small time bar band, disbanding after a few years for Lennon and McCartney to begin lifelong careers in the steel industry? Would Bill Gates have ended up repairing pinball machines for a living? I don't think so. Granted, certain specific things fell into place for them to end up where they did, but if things had happened differently I think they still would have risen to the top one way or another. They were smart enough, they worked hard, and if they wanted it bad enough, I think they would have made their way around whatever got in their way. These \\"Outliers\\" did not stumble upon success accidentally - they worked hard for it, and I feel they would have reached it one way or another despite missing one or more of Gladwell's criteria. Ultimately I think that this interpretation of Gladwell's book is very hopeful to many people. If you are smart enough and work harder than others, you can make your own way. Sure luck and being in the right place at the right time helps, but there is also such a thing as making your own luck.<br /><br />Outliers is subtitled \\"The Story of Success.\\" Maybe it should be titled \\"Stories of Success\\" instead, because although these may be (Gladwell's) ways to achieve success, they are certainly not the only ways.or more of Gladwell's criteria. Ultimately I think that this interpretation of Gladwell's book is very hopeful to many people. If you are smart enough and work harder than others, you can make your own way. Sure luck and being in the right place at the right time helps, but there is also such a thing as making your own luck.    Outliers is subtitled \\"The Story of Success.\\" Maybe it should be titled \\"Stories of Success\\" instead, because although these may be (Gladwell's) ways to achieve success, they are certainly not the only ways.

1416596585|4.0|I bought this book automatically because I had previously read and enjoyed Levy's previous works: Insanely Great, Hackers and Chaos. Given his heritage covering technology companies and personalities as both an author and a journalist, I was curious what he would make of Google.<br /><br />The book is expansive and provides a lot of additional colour around Google, some of which I found of interest as I had worked at Yahoo! competing against Google and working with some of the early darlings of the web 2.0 movement - Flickr and Delicious. There were a couple of things that surprised me such as Google's use of machine learning on areas like translation explained why grammar is still so bad in this area as it needs heuristics that lexicographers could provide similar to that offered by Crystal Semantics.<br /><br />Overall it was interesting to see that as with most large organisations Google is not only fallible but run through with realpolitik and a fair bit of serendipity. This contrasts with the external perception of Google as the technological &Uuml;bermensch. A classic example of this is the series of missteps Google made whilst competing in China, which are documented in the book. From staffing practices, promotional tactics and legal to technology; Google blew it's chances and Baidu did a better job.<br /><br />As an aside it was interesting to note that Google used queries on rival search engines to try and work out how to comply with Chinese government regulations, which is eerily like bad practices that Google accused Bing of last February in `hiybbprqag'-gate.<br /><br />There is a curious myopia that runs through a lot of later Google product thinking that reminded me of the reality and perceptions that I was aware of existing inside Microsoft from the contact I have had with the organisation through the various different agencies I have worked at. A classic example of this is the Google view of a file-less future, which by implication assumes that people won't have legacy documents or use services other than the Google cloud. It is a myopia that comes part of arrogance and a patronising attitude towards the consumer that Google always knows best about every aspect of their needs.<br /><br />Contrast this with Apple and iTunes. Whilst Apple would like to sell you only content from the iTunes store, it recognises that you will have content from different sources: Amazon MP3s, ripped CDs, podcasts and self-created files that iTunes needs to play nicely with.<br /><br />The `no files' approach assumes ubiquitous bandwidth which is likely to be a fiction for a while. (Part of the reason why I am able to write this post is that I was stuck for half-a-day on a train journey to Wales enjoying patchy mobile phone coverage and a wi-fi free environment, which allowed me to focus on reading this book in hardback).  This approach smacks of the old data lock-in that Microsoft used to have with proprietary file formats for its Office documents.<br /><br />Levy does a good job pulling all of this together and chronicling Google, but he fails to cast a critical eye over it all. I suspect that this is because he is too close to the company: the access that he gained enveloped him. Which is a shame as all the experience and insight Levy could bring to the book that would add value to the reader is omitted. Whilst In The Plex is an interesting historical document, it could be so much more.services other than the Google cloud. Sure, I could nitpick all of the prequels and Jedi all day, but I still like them.  My least favorite films in this series end up being Jedi and Phantom Menace.  But onto the actual product.....<br /><br />As far as transfers alone this set deserves FIVE STARS.  These movies have never looked better.  Really.  It's unreal. Ben Burt's sound design really shines here. The puppetry by Frank Oz was great as always but the look was all wrong down from material for skin to the lighting.  He looked like a cheesy puppet, which the original trilogy's Yoda really truly didn't.  Honestly, this brings another factor to my attention. We'll see....<br /><br />Okay, and now onto the three special features discs.  This is my major complaint regarding this set and this is why I didn't give it a full five stars which it deserved. Especially the doc for Phantom Menace which had a doc regarding the making of that film and it was very intersting and entertaining.<br /><br />Okay, disc seven of the bonus material is all about the prequel trilogy.  It's sparse as far as running time goes, but adds up when you break down the interactive material which consists of concept art, stroyboards, animatics, and turn table vies of vehicles and characters.  Cool stuff, but I garuntee you'll only watch it once. Seeing the concept art here is wonderful due to the fact that they are so old.  It's great to have this stuff on file finally, but the real meat of the original trilogy comes into play with disc 9.<br /><br />Disc 9 has a great archival collection of features released back in the 70's and 80's and as recent as 2010!  The Making of Star Wars(back when it was simply called Star Wars) is wonderful, if slightly more truncating and missing retrospect when compared to Empire of Dreams, runs about 49 minutes and covers all the prodution details but, for obvious reasons, doesn't go into the hardships taken on by Lucas.  Empire Strikes Back: SPFX runs 48 minutes and basically just sticks with the visual effects aspect of this film.  While, again, missing intimate details regarding the production and behind the scenes stuff you sure get to know how that film's ingenious visuals were realized.  Classic Creatures: Return of the Jed runs 48 minutes and is my favorite of the original film's vintage docs.  It just fouces on the practical creatures and puppetry heavily employed for this sequel.  I don't know why but I still get a kick out of men and women building, operating and deseigning practical creature effects.  Repeat info regarding Jabba's creation comes from Empire of Dreams but it doesn't do that in other areas, and it's hosted by Carrie Fisher and Billy Dee Williams.  Anatomy of a Dewback is a 26 minute doc I remember watching back when the original films were being re-released in their special edition form back in 1997.  It basically painstakingly goes over the restoration of the original trilogy, and the scene that was added with the Storm Troopers riding the dewbacks in the desert.  Lucas gives his reasons, regarding asthetic and storytelling for this addition.  This is the only new secene covered in this doc, but it is really fun.  Star Warriors is a feature length doc running 84 minutes about a Star Wars club that has 'movie authentic' costumes that they do free charity work in.  It's about their trek to getting a march in a big Rose Bowl parade.  YAAAAAAAAAAWN.  Star Wars Tech is a 46 minute doc straight from TV regarding Star Wars technology and how fact based it is, and how possible it is to become true, and how much of it actually DID come true.  Very cool.  A Conversation with the Masters: The Empire Strikes Back 30 Years later is a 2010 doc only running 25 minutes but has new interviews with Lucas, director Irvin Kershner and screenwriter Lawrence Kasdan reagarding the most loved of the original trilogy.  Very neat, especially hearing from Kasdan, but it mostly repeats ino discussed in Empire of Dreams.<br /><br />Finally, the last new special feature is a 97 minute compilation of various Star Wars references and parodies from over the years in movies and TV.  We get clips from Robot Chicken and Family Guy(of course!), but we also get clips from Clerks, Fanboys, The Donnie and Marie Show, many various seasons of Saturday Night Live(fav being the lost audition tapes for A New Hope starring Kevin Spacey doing dead on and hysterical impressions of Christopher Walken and Jack Lemon), How I Met Your Mother, and many others.  Funny enough, but you can find these clips easily on the net, and honestly this, and the Star Warriors, feature I would easily trade in for a Hi-Def version of Empire of Dreams and ALL the prequel DVD's special features.<br /><br />The one retained special feature from the dvd's is all six films' commentary tracks, which are great!!!  The best info you'll get about the films.  There is a new commentary track added to each film which mixes the original commentaries with archive interviews with various producers, FX guys, and the actors.  I've only listened to Sith for this, and it's good, but I would rather have had the interviews seperated.  Also, some of the snippets on this commentary are repeats from the interviews presented in the special features discs.<br /><br />So, there you have it.  It's a mixed bag.  If you're an uber fan, but not a total dweeb, like me you'll want to hold onto your bonus discs from the previous releases unfortunately.  I'm sure we'll being seeing more Star Wars on blu ray in my lifetime, probably three times more.  The transfers and sound make it worth and some of the special features.  Really, the real question comes down to this: why have the orginal bonus materials not been included on the very roomy blu ray discs, especially when there are 9 discs?  And, of course, WHERE THE HELL ARE THE REMASTERED HI-DEF COPIES OF THE UNALTERED ORIGINAL TRILOGY?????  It baffles me with sooooo many fanboys asking for this that Lucas still hasn't given it.  Why?  At least for posterities sake this should happen.  I'm sure it will one day.......<br /><br />PS: If anyone out there knows of any alterations to these films that I missed, please let me know in the comments section.f features released back in the 70's and 80's and as recent as 2010! B00007G1ZC|4.0|I love the personal touches that are given to `Dreams', a film inspired by the actual dreams of the film's director, Akira Kurosawa.  Kurosawa made some astonishing films, and `Dreams' may be his most visually impactful.  It is, essentially, eight short films strung together through similar themes.  From the first short film (my favorite in some ways) to the final, stunning (visually and emotionally) punch to the heart, Kurosawa understands that these short films tell a tale that is important to hear and absorb.  As a whole, the collection works very nicely.  In parts, some stories work better than others and a few can feel stretched beyond necessity.  Maintaining an ethereal atmosphere (thus, the dream reference) throughout the entire film, `Dreams' truly lives up to its name.  From the epic depictions of fog layered hill tops (in `The Weeping Demon') to colorful adaptations of famous paintings (in the visual feast, `Crows'), `Dreams' will keep you glued to every frame; but it's the profound statement being made by the core of these dreams that will keep you thinking about it time and time again.  Delving into themes that concern death, loss, guilt and the separation of man and nature, Kurosawa has created something unforgettable.

B001U0HBPG|5.0|I haven't read the book prior to seeing the movie which is something I don't normally do. So, I don't know how faithful this adoption is. However, I really enjoyed watching this film.<br /><br />The story of Benjamin Button is sweet, different, unique and eventually very sad. I guess that is inevitable for someone who was born very different from the rest of us. I'm not going to retell the plot as I'm sure a lot of reviewers did so masterfully, I'm going to say the reason why I liked this movie despite my distaste for *sad* and *depressing* endings without giving too much away.<br /><br />What made this story stand out for me isn't just the unique and mysterious circumstances surrounding Benjamin's birth but the moral behind the story. We live in a world that is fascinated with Youth and Beauty so much to the point of obsession. In a world where Cosmetic Surgeries and anti aging products are multi/billion if not trillion trades I think many people with gladly give everything they have/own to be in the shoes of Benjamin Button. Imagine growing Younger instead of older ? Imagine becoming more beautiful and healthy by the day instead of the other way round. I know it is impossible but if it were wouldn't you take it ?<br /><br />What I like about this film is that it keeps things realistic, It shows the *bad* side of getting younger. The side that the media doesn't show, the side that people don't know about or pay attention to or take for granted. Imagine if you were getting younger and younger by the year and everyone around is getting older. You won't be able to see your kids grow into adults, you won't be able to attend their graduations, school activities, or even weddings because by that time you'll be a child. You won't be able to age gracefully, see your grandchildren or die peacefully in your sleep as an old man/woman. I know many people wouldn't consider dying as an infant in their quest of getting younger but lets face it people aren't immortal and the only way to be forever young is to die in your youth.<br /><br />Just the idea of retiring to teenage hood and childhood instead of prime age and elderly is very exhausting to me. All the things that you'll have to sacrifice might not be worth it. I'm sure Benjamin Button wished he could be like the rest of his peers and age gracefully instead of the other way round but he had to submit and be content by his share, he rarely if ever complained about it and I admire his character a lot for that. I know there was a lot of implying on it but I was hoping that the author would somehow manage to make poor Benjamin age normally so he could live the rest of his life with the woman he loves and watch his daughter grow but still I couldn't complain because despite it being sad and depressing it does reflect the message it is trying to send.<br /><br />The Cast choice is perfect (IMO) I'm not a fan of Brad Pitt but this has to be one of my favorite movies by him along with Seven Years in Tibet. Cate Blanchett is a superb actress and I really liked her role here. This is the second Pitt/Blanchett collaboration and I liked it better than Babel.<br /><br />I recommend this movie for anyone who likes drama or is looking for something different. 0441013597|5.0|It's hard to add anything to what's been said about Frank Herbert's \\"Dune\\" in the 45 years since it first appeared. \\"Dune\\" was already a classic when i read it in 1981, and unlike many SF books from the cusp of speculative fiction's New Wave, its impact remains as timeless now as it did then. Herbert grounded his sprawling tale of imperial politics and ecological revolution in a character story worthy of Tolstoy, downplaying the nuts-and-bolts aspects of his milieu's technology in a way that prevents \\"Dune\\" from seeming stale, even today.<br /><br />As with many of the most seminal works of speculative fiction and fantasy, the most amazing thing about \\"Dune\\" is how close it came to never seeing print, having been passed over by twenty publishers before being initially picked up by a nonfiction small press. In the canon of F&SF, there are few books whose importance literally cannot be understated. \\"Dune\\" is one of those. Without it, the world of imaginative literature would not be the same.<br /><br />I break with a lot of Herbert fans in my complete dispassion for the later \\"Dune\\" books, including the capstone of the original trilogy, \\"Children of Dune\\". To anyone who hasn't read the books, my recommendation is always to read \\"Dune\\" and \\"Dune Messiah\\" back to back as one continuous narrative, with the sequel bringing Herbert's vision to a satisfying and heartbreaking end.

B005J4ASDG|4.0|Firstly, this isn't a review of the music itself, but of the structure of the Symphony and the performance of the musicians.  I assume anybody reading this already has knowledge and appreciation for Howard Shore's music, which on its own terms is 5-star, must-own stuff.<br /><br />Now that's out of the way, what of the Symphony?  To call it a symphony is something of a stretch, since it doesn't follow the structure of a traditional classical symphony.  Basically, The Lord of the Rings Symphony is a very long, comprehensive suite representing music from the scores of all three films.  There are six movements; two movements apiece for each of the films.  Consider it a highlight reel of the best moments from the scores.  Nothing will ever replace hearing the scores in their gargantuan entirety, but listening to the Symphony is a wonderful experience on its own.  Although many may have preferred a true 'symphony' in the classical sense, organized by theme and counterpoint, etc., that seems somewhat illogical given the subject material.  Instead, the Symphony follows the narrative structure and order of the films.  It's like Cliffs Notes for the original scores.  Frankly, the work would be far more appropriately called a suite, similar to a lengthy ballet suite, but oh well, it's the content and not the title that's important.  Also, if you or someone else you know has previously managed to avoid hearing Shore's music, the Symphony would be a great introduction.<br /><br />As far as the performances themselves, I was skeptical considering I haven't previously heard of the 21st Century Symphony Orchestra (the name sounds cheesy), nor of Ludwing Wicki (conductor).  I was fortunate enough to attend a concert with Shore himself conducting the Atlanta Symphony Orchestra and Chorus, and the results were unforgettable.  Needless to say I had very high expectations for an eventual recording of the Symphony, but much to my dismay, one was never released despite the release of the Complete Recordings and the Rarities Archive.  That makes this recording an unexpected surprise, timed to coincide with the 10th anniversary of the first film.  Thankfully, my concerns were assuaged.  The performance of the 90-piece, German-based orchestra and around 100-strong semi-professional chorus was fabulous.  The brass in particular rose spectacularly to the occasion.  Even the soloists did their best to imitate the originals with acceptable results.  Thanks to maestro Wicki, the pacing is accurate and clean, though at times it can seem a little rushed.  Again, probably nothing will best the original performances by the London Philharmonic, but these performances often compare favorably to the interpretations of on-album excerpts by the City of Prague Philharmonic and Cincinnati Pops.  The only real knock I give this recording is that, as a result (I assume) of being recorded live, the sound quality suffers a bit.  That's not to say it's bad (it's actually decent), but the stereo separation doesn't feel as immersive and it's more difficult to pick out individual instruments.  Although audience noise is thankfully limited, there are two moments of applause on the recording - the first is after the second movement, and the second is after the last movement.  Keep in mind this is a review of the digital album I purchased on Amazon, but I imagine the standard CD listening experience would be very much the same.<br /><br />All in all, purchase this recording if you have any appreciation for Shore's magnum opus!  I guarantee you'll be glad you did.  I hope more recordings of the Symphony by other well-known orchestras will follow.Complete Recordings and the Rarities Archive. That's not to say it's bad (it's actually decent), but the stereo separation doesn't feel as immersive and it's more difficult to pick out individual instruments. Those reviewers are right, they are not included, and I will say that I believe the set benefits from it.  The saga feels like it all connects, like it is one story of six films rather than two separate sagas of varying quality.  And Vader's new line at the Emperor's demise doesn't change anything.  It doesn't make him 'whiny' as so many are claiming, and will only ruin the scene if you allow it to.  I actually forgot about the new Krayt dragon call until my gf reminded me of it.    First, let's look at the content of the films.  All six star wars films stand out from the cinematic crowd in almost every way.  The costumes, design, sets, creatures, ships, etc. are all incredible to look at and really stand out in Blu-Ray.  You'll notice art on the walls of Theed palace and wonder what sort of history that piece has for their planet, and see the scratches on Plo Koon's breather.  The creativity and artistry that have gone into all six films is mind-blowing.  As is the overarching story.  I've heard a lot of people say \\"Always watch 4 - 6 first.\\"  I'd disagree with that and say watch them in the order they're packaged and intended to be seen, 1 - 6.  Anakin becomes a much more tragic character and you'll watch 4-6 and your heart will break a little bit at his unmasking.  I was a prequel hater for a long time, but I must say that it's been 11 years since Episode I and it's time to give these movies another chance.  They really are phenomenal films, despite the acting and dialogue's stiff moments - but even the originals have some eye-roll worthy lines, so it just kind of adds to the character of the films.  It's not the Dark Knight, it's Star Wars - a samurai-western-sci-fi-fantasywith muppets.  And that is what makes it awesome.    One thing I noticed is that the originals are shot in a much more critical focus than the prequels, which does affect the way they display in HD.  There seems to be a lot more to see in the prequels, the screen estate is occupied with various eye catching designs, costumes, etc and it almost looks real.  Much of the originals are shot in critical, so the back and fore ground become more blurry.  This is more of a comment than a criticism, the focal range is part of the film's artistry, but it does create a visual discontinuity in production style.  The originals do look incredible in HD.  You can see all of Chewie's hair (and a few strays of Tarkin's), the texture of Ben's robe, etc.  They look like real people in your living room.  And the soundtrack is wondrous - getting a perfect rumble without ever peaking.    Finally, and I haven't gotten to look at all of them yet, but the special features are totally packed with content.  Each episode is divided into planets, showing the production of that particular part of the film, and within each planet are deleted scenes, concept art, interviews (which I thoroughly enjoyed and I believe are brand new), and some rotating maquettes that really show off the interactivity of your Blu Ray player.  One *slight* criticism is that there is no way to watch ALL the deleted scenes at once.  You can only watch each film's deleted scenes within each planet.  I understand this for the sake of organization, because there is SO MUCH that it would become a mess of features if it weren't split up somehow, but I did find myself getting a little lost in the menus at times.  On top of all that, there's 8 or 9 documentaries from over the years about various production aspects, and a 90 minute compilation of spoofs from all different media.  I'm really looking forward to that one.    If you're a fan of the saga, or are looking for something to really show off your home theater system, thisis a worthy investment.  I already own these movies in 3 or 4 different incarnations, but this set blows them all away and was definitely worth the price of admission.  I will get enjoyment out of this for years to come.  Thanks, Lucasfilm.

B001F290E4|5.0|Seal burst upon the world in 1991 with a global hit album SEAL that garnered three top-20 hit singles and two more top-40 hits, clearly better than half of a nine track album full of unique compositions with style, lyrical, tempo, and key changes.  Though slotted often as a \\"soul/R&B\\" album, SEAL and its follow-up also called SEAL, which garnered three additional top-20 hits, including the Batman Forever soundtrack standout Kiss From A Rose, are really far from traditional Soul or R&B.  His writing and performing style more closely resembled the experiment post-wave rock of Tears For Fears and others.  After three smash albums, Seal's popularity in the USA began to decline with smaller and smaller sales, even though in European markets the diminishing returns were less notable.  Seal continued through this last decade to be a top-notch performer despite the more lackluster sales compared to SEAL, SEAL (II), and HUMAN BEING.<br /><br />In 2008, Seal joined forces with \\"Hit Man\\" David Foster to produce an album comprised entirely of cover songs.  Not just any cover songs but covers of literally the greatest soul-R&B singles in the canon of a genre.  These classics are embedded in the brains of nearly every baby boomer who ever turned on a radio, bought 45 rpm singles, attended a high school dance, entered a bar with a jukebox, or gathered at the late evening drive-in.  The project at face value sounded like something doomed to failure from the get-go.  But one has to bear in mind that this was a superstar with a terrific voice, and a man whose prowess as producer, arranger, and talent manager is unparalleled.  It was literally a marriage made in heaven, so the choice of material was a fully loaded intercontinental ballistic missile.  I had my fears, do not doubt that for a second!  But once you listen to the first track, the song he performed nationally on nearly every mid-day to late night talk show, morning news and chat show, and in prime time on the television show Eli Stone, the song that entered the charts as a hit single on adult contemporary \\"Smooth Jazz\\" radio and worked its way up R&B/Hip Hop charts, the immortal Sam Cooke's A Change Is Gonna Come, once you hear it if you haven't already, then you know this is going to be a Killer album.  Up front I will say it is not a \\"perfect\\" album, but it is a darned good one!<br /><br />The only way I can honestly review this album is by means of a method I really dislike using, but in this case, a song by song analysis is truly in order.  I want to give you a well balanced report on this album which I purchased from Amazon the day it came out and have been listening to for almost three years now.  Why did I wait so long?  Honestly I am way behind in my cue of reviews and given the time I have waited, I have listened to this album enough now to provide a solid balanced report rather than a knee-jerk reaction.  Had I written this even two years ago, I might have said that only half the album was worthy.  But I have since been able to compare the originals, other covers, and done so numerous times by just listening to my own music collection.  Honestly this is an album you can purchase, rip it to your portable player and listen to these songs individually as they come up in any random play mode, or mix into a homemade CD without having to listen to the entire CD to appreciate.  You can though, pop this CD into the car stereo as I have done many times and allow it to accompany you to work or back or both.  The question to ponder is how is Seal's voice on these songs?  Don't bother asking about David Foster's arrangements and production, the man has magic spirits that turn pretty much everything he touches into gold, no matter who the end audience is.  So let's take a close look at the material.<br /><br />A Change Is Gonna Come.  Sam Cooke's triumphant civil rights anthem has become so highly acclaimed it is ranked #12 on Rolling Stone's 500 Greatest Songs Of All Time. I personallyhave counted no less than 70 different artists who have covered this song since the original release and I am sure there are more \\"less popular\\" recordings out there.  Seal has both the vocal power and the proper timbre in his voice to sing this song and make it sound as great as the original, especially given today's recording technology.  Add to that David Foster's powerful orchestral arrangement and you have a giant recording!<br /><br />I Can't Stand The Rain.  Originally by Ann Peebles, this 1973 hit was named by John Lennon as \\"the best song ever\\".  One look at the way it dominated the various charts all over the world might prove him right.  It was in the top 20 of 18 different countries, and in 14 of those was in the top 10.  In 1984 Tina Turner included it on her PRIVATE DANCER album and it found its way into the charts of the USA, UK, Germany, and France.  The other truly great cover of this song is by Angeline Ball in the film (and on the soundtrack of the film) The Commitments.  Seal and Foster submit a straight forward rendition here with an excellent rendering of the song to a male vocal performance.  Not powerful but definitely pleasant.<br /><br />It's A Man's Man's Man's World.  James Brown's incredible #1 hit single of 1966 was called \\"almost biblically chauvinistic\\" by Rolling Stone magazine and those words have resounded around the song forever.  Regardless of how you feel about the song, and despite that the song infers within itself that it is all due to the woman, the performance has never been rivaled by a cover artist, save for possibly Joss Stone at The Kennedy Center in 2003.  Seal could have drowned himself in this song had it not been for the super-power orchestral backing of Foster.  His delivery is punchy as it should be.  To call it a straight cover would be sophomoric, since how else could the song be delivered.  The only question is does the artist have the chops to make it sound great?  Seal does!  The band and the orchestra bring this one to a crescendo with a proper James Brown fade out, Seal delivering the obligatory \\"lost in the wilderness...in his bitterness\\" nuances.  Remember this song was co-written with a woman and had to include her female observations.  That should at least calm some of those who would sully to call this song dated.<br /><br />Here I Am.  This is the first of two classics by \\"The Reverend\\" Al Green offered to us by Seal.  Taken from Green's masterpiece, Call Me, this single was his 3rd most popular song ever following close on the heels of Let's Stay Together and I'm Still In Love With You (also represented on this album).  Most of Seal's translations of these classics follow on his normal register, but in dealing with Al Green, Seal demonstrates a fine ability to soften up his vocal chords and raise the timbre close enough to an octave within reach of Al Green's unique voice.  Here I Am for the most part stays within the caliber of Seals normal range but he will stretch that for the next Al Green classic and marvelously well.  As with all these recordings, Foster arranges a tremendous backing on this.<br /><br />I've Been Loving You Too Long.  This Otis Redding single has more stature to it than it originally received as a single, a minor hit reaching #15 on the R&B charts and only clocked in at #85 on the Billboard pop singles.  Fans of this song grew with time and Rolling Stone magazine rank it as #110 on their 500 Greatest Songs Of All Time.  Seal gives this song a deserving rendition with spectacular respect to the author and Foster treats it rightly so with the instrumentation to keep it a blues based R&B single with just the right amount of orchestration.  The Rolling Stones covered this song with one of the greatest treatments, so fine that Otis returned the compliment by covering Satisfaction!  The all time best cover is however, without a doubt, Tina Turner's (with Ike) in 1968.  Not bad by Seal, above average.<br /><br />It's Alright.  The legendary Curtis Mayfield wrote and recorded this song in 1963 with his group The Impressions and it was a chart topper.  The best news for Seal is that It's Alright has not suffered from over-exposure by having countless covers.  This allows the song to be rendered very nicely by Seal with little emotional effect to the contrary.  In other words, you can listen to it without being reminded of other (better) artists, and in that respect, it ends up being thought of as one of the better performances on the CD.  But honestly that is only because of ear-prejudiced and if you can somehow shut out the instinct to compare, you would find that there are probably a half dozen songs on this album which are much better than this one.  It still deserves kudos though.  This is the first of two Mayfield tunes which Seal will work on this album.<br /><br />If You Don't Know Me By Now.  This is my choice for weak choice on this album.  Not that Seal does not have the vocal chops to close it, but even with Foster's delicate orchestral arrangements and piano work, Seal's rendition really adds nothing new nor unforgettable to the moment.  A Change Is Gonna Come, Man's World, Stand By Me, and even People Get Ready, famous as they are still tower way above this cover.  The trouble is that some serious vocalists have torched this song since the original Harold Melvin & The Blue Notes released this top of the charts hit single.  Gamble and Huff wrote the song for Patti LaBelle and her group Labelle but it was left \\"for later\\" and then when Teddy Pendergrass and company took it to the cleaners, well who was going to try and one-up that?  Almost two decades later an artist I dearly love, Simply Red, again sailed the ship to Number One with a rendition so heartfelt and emotional that it is difficult for me to acknowledge that Seal does do a fine job on the song.  It is just that after Pendergrass and Simply Red soared on this one love song with vocals to make a linebacker sob, the work by Seal leaves me thinking, isn't that nice.  Well \\"nice\\" makes not a spectacular thing.<br /><br />Knock On Wood.  Eddie Floyd's R&B rocker is a sort of concert staple for all kind of genre performers.  Otis Redding performed the first cover of this classic and since then, there have been nearly two dozen that I either own or at least have heard more than once!  Wilson Pickett, Buddy Guy, Harpers Bizarre, Ike & Tina Turner, Cher, Eric Clapton, Jeff Healey, Michael Bolton (yeah even him), James Taylor, just to name a few, all have hammered the tree with Knock On Wood.  Seal adds one more to the mix, a solid funky version performed well but again not spectacular and honestly another cover choice might have suited better than a song which even in its original form was over classified as a \\"classic\\".  But then that's just me.  You might love it!  I for one have grown tired of this song and so I can only be impartial by admitting that Seal does a good job and Foster as well.  Produced well, sung well, blah blah...<br /><br />I'm Still In Love With You.  Okay, now we are returning to the fold!  The second Al Green single included on this set was the right choice!  In fact Seal uses pitch and timbre here to perform a nice emulation of the original and it works quite well.  It is nuanced and phrased just right and you will be so glad to hear this song after the last two, you might boot this one right up to your number one favorite!  Wonderful, even delightful, instrumentation and vocals.  Despite the fact that this is perhaps Al Greens most famous song, one that everyone on earth adores, Seal sends it right down perfection alley, another home run.<br /><br />Free.  The second hit by a female R&B artist covered in this collection, Free was a '76 funk single by Deniece Williams.  This is perhaps the least commonly known song on the set and that works well in Seal's favor.  I for one even forgot who was responsible for the original hit and had to look it up!  Again, great instrumentation, arrangement, and vocals build up the case for Seals album after emerging from a shallow pool created by two or three lesser treatments in the middle of the set.  This song sets up the ending which goes out as big as the album opened up...<br /><br />Stand By Me.  Another of the most covered songs in the known universe, Ben E King's immortal classic has hit the charts so many times it is nearly impossible to count.*  My personal favorite cover of this song is by John Lennon of course, who took it to the singles charts from his ROCK & ROLL album.  His delivery is uniquely Lennon, gruffing the vocals for pure rock essence.  BMI, who keep track of such things, report that Stand By Me is the 4th most performed song of the 20th century, with something in the vicinity of 7 million performances.  Seal has the vocals to cover Ben E King with pride and the real power in this song comes from Foster who has the orchestra punch our lights out with swells of crescendo reminiscent of what he did for Michael Bubl&eacute;'s \\"Feeling Good\\".  You can't argue with a good thing and this song is the goodest thing on this album after the first track!<br /><br />People Get Ready.  A second entry from Curtis Mayfield's days with The Impressions closes SOUL.  Seal makes as good a choice closing the album (or rather Foster probably does that for him) as he did opening it.  With a huge boom, Seal grabbed us with one of the greatest anthems of all time.  Now he is going to put us to bed with one of the greatest spirituals of all time.  Rolling Stone picked People Get Ready as #24 on their list of the Greatest Songs Of All Time.  A panel of 20 songwriter producers, including Paul McCartney and Brian Wilson chose it for inclusion in the Top 10 Best Songs Of All Time.  Foster makes sure that the instrumentation on the song is both a tribute to and a hymnal nod to Curtis Mayfield by including his signature guitar break sound in Seal's cover.  This song, I have and know of over 40 different covers.  Unlike some others on this set and more like Stand By Me, these last two immortal God Songs which close the album, have so much resonance in our society that another cover does not \\"hurt\\" the song nor this album.  The question is whether or not, again, Seal stands up to the task, and just as with Stand By Me, Seal and Foster nail this one to the wall.  Thank you Seal for this spiritual delight, your delivery is as kind to the song as was Bob Marley's cover of it!<br /><br />*there are ostensibly over 400 recorded versions of Stand By Me.  Notable hit single covers of it are Spyder Turner's '67 hit in which he performs impressions of various R&B hit-makers singing the main verse in their style, Mickey Gilley's 1980 #1 Country hit cover from the Urban Cowboy soundtrack, Maurice White (of Earth Wind & Fire) had a top-10 R&B hit in '85, Prince Royce turned it into a #1 Latin single hit in 2010, John Lennon's '75 cover went to the top-20, and let's remember that Ben E King's original hit the charts twice: the original went to #1 in 1961, and then it was re-released as a single in conjunction with the Rob Reiner/Stephen King movie Stand By Me in 1986 when it peaked at #4 on the billboard charts.  Jimi Hendrix, Otis Redding, and Elton John all have fantastic cover recordings of this monumental song, and finally, for a couple of odd takes, Muhammad Ali included it on his 1963 album I AM THE GREATEST, and Stephen King and Warren Zevon recorded it together for a charity album STRANGER THAN FICTION in 1998<br /><br />I know this has been a long review, but if you are wanting to read an objective and decisive review for a \\"questionable\\" album (it has received \\"mixed\\" critical reviews, some rave about it, some torture it with the same glee they would devote to small animals) then I wanted to give that to you.  Your choice in the end.  My advice is buy it and enjoy it.  It is a Heart Full Of Soul.  If it were not for the stupendous performances on A Change Is Gonna Come, It's A Man's World, Stand By Me, and People Get Ready, this album would only warrant 4 Stars from me, but those four songs launch the CD as a whole into the atmosphere despite a couple lack-luster tracks that only seem lack-luster next to the more inspired tracks.  Go ahead.  This is Seal's best album since Human Being, and even if you are not an aficionado of that Seal era, if you are a lover of Soul and R&B hits, this is the CD you want.  Other artists (Michael McDonald for one) have gone to bat with this format and come up short.  This one is a homer with three men on base.Knock On Wood. I will advise viewers that haven't seen the show from this point on that, although all the elements that make this show great are there, the show really starts to lose a little bit of it's charm as Rory and Lorelai spend less time together. The mother and daughter really start to have separate story lines, and this can be kind of bumpy. However, when they are together (and getting along) it is perfect.    This is my favorite show of all time, and I still miss it. I have all the seasons, and watch it with friends who haven't seen the show all the time. It's undeniable charm and fast paced wit wins over anyone who can keep up. And if you think you know pop culture, this show will test your knowledge. I have many times paused the show to Google a name, or event reference that left me saying, \\"What!?!\\"    If you already love this show, I recommend Reba and I Love Lucy. Both of these shows have great elements of Gilmore Girls. (Don't blow off the Reba show just because you are not a country music fan. I don't care for her music, but she has one of the most natural comedic timing I have seen since Lucy. B0054N73EY|4.0|I'm With You is a really good record but it is not a classic RHCP album. New guitarist, new vibe and the fact that the boys are getting older. They have given us a musical variety here and show off their talents. It's like when the Clash came out with London Calling--purists hated it. But now, it is a modern classic. No overt politics here (good!) but the mix of styles is exciting. Old time funk, salsa and even a beat borrowed from the Culture Club.    I would also say that there are shades of early U2 here. From the lyrics to the energy to the sparse, spacey guitar. It's like they borrowed Steve Lillywhite and Brian Eno for a few days to get that haunting sound that dominated the best U2 albums. (U2 lost their  mojo after Zooropa in my opinion.)    Check out the new and improved RHCP. You will enjoy the ride!

B005GSY23M|5.0|Drive is an experience to be had. I waited to see the film before I reviewed the score because I felt like I needed to experience the sound and image of this film together. Drive is a pure adrenaline rush of a film. Nicolas Winding Refn has crafted a supreme thriller. The music of the film is the most important bonding element of this violent and suspenseful tale. The film is filled with stunning imagery and long duration takes. It also features a seamless soundscape between score and song.<br /><br />Drive has a vintage feel. It feels as if it were made in the 70's but remodeled for modern day. Very much like a beautifully restored vintage car. The character of the driver feels as if he was plucked right out from a spaghetti western. He barely speaks and dons a jacket with a scorpion on the back (very much like El Mariachi's jacket in Desperado). The song choices will be the first thing that the audiences will pay attention to. The songs selected fit the same thematic feel of the movie. They sound like they are 80's hits but are all modern songs with a vivid techno pulse. Cliff Martinez then crafts his stunning score around this universe. The music lacks any graspable structure and is a sonic wash of electronic tones. The way it works in the film is absolutely stunning. I can only describe the music as colorful. There was a point in the film where Ryan Gosling was driving and the sequence was just him driving at night. The shot composition was tight with shallow focus so all you saw was blurs of colors. At that point I felt a rush as the music took over and all you heard were these tones of the night. Cool and crisp, I was able to close my eyes for just a few seconds and see distinct colors as the tones shifted. Martinez also plays the emotions heavy with the relationship between the driver, Irene and Benicio. The score crafts an incredible bubbling tension as well. The way it works with the editing of certain scenes is amazing. Refn is also unafraid to let silence play. A lot of times you will have pockets of pure silence that are broken with a startling sound. The sound design is impeccable and Martinez knows when music is not needed at all. All in all this is a masterful, bold and unique experience.<br /><br />Drive is a rare example of electronic tones working to perfection with the colors and shot composition of the film. 0316067598|3.0|Though Mr. Luttrell has a unique story and a perspective that most of us will never gain first-hand, this book is marred by poor writing. Many small facts and details are repeated in different parts of the book when one mention would have sufficed, while other things, like the author's age at the time of the events, are not given. Certain important topics (like ROE, public perception of the military, media coverage of armed conflict, the history of the Pashtun people) are not condensed into cohesive discussions or examples of his points. Instead, more about these topics keeps showing up after they had been dealt with earlier. Or the reverse occured, with a tiny unanchored statement made, then not fleshed out until much later. These, unfortunately, give the flavor of a rant to what is, underneath, important material.      The first half of the book jumps jerkily from topic to topic: from childhood vignettes, to background information about the war, to the ins and outs of SEAL training, to the problems with today's liberal media, to what is visibile out the window of the helicopter, to the author's comraderie with the men he serves with, to the equipment his carrying, to what he and his friends think of the president. It feels cut and pieced together in a way that is distracting and reads like an unfinished product. Once Operation Redwing gets under way, the story stays more focused, but still strays at times. Why the rather extraordinary history and experience of this man weren't given a more rigorous streamlining by his experienced co-author and editor, I'll never know.     Lastly, I didn't feel there was a satisfactory explanation for why Luttrell was so sure the three goatherds had betrayed his team's presence. Since this was a pivotal point in how he felt about the outcome of the mission and all that followed, it should have been stated more directly. They each offer a slightly different setup, and after reading a ton of reviews online, I decided to buy the B-Grip. After a couple of months and a couple dozen photoshoots, I wasn't happy. It distributed the weight in a manner that was very uncomfortable to me.<br /><br />Just last month, I folded my hand and purchased the SpiderPro Holster... WOW!  This belt solution is amazing and exactly what I needed. It is just totally awesome! Hopefully you find this review helpful. The area where the camera rests against you is stiff enough so you don't feel anything. It distributes the weight of my 6-9 pound camera evenly across my waist and hips. B0034G4P8A|4.0|With Kenneth Branagh at the helm, it's hard not to draw comparisons between THOR and various Shakespearean tragedies: brash, arrogant son defies his father, bringing about apocalyptic consequences, only to find that he was betrayed by those whom he trusted most.  It's an age-old story, which works in THOR's favor: it lends the film a firm sense of ethos, a grand backdrop which Branagh manipulates with great skill.<br /><br />It helps that he's chosen a fine cast.  Chris Hemsworth has solid screen presence; the film sags a bit when Thor loses his powers, but picks up admirably when he regains them.  Throughout, Hemsworth remains, at his worst, watchable, and at his best, thoroughly engaging.  As the devious Loki, Tom Hiddleston is a devious, manipulating wonder (stick around after the credits for what is arguably his best scene).  Anthony Hopkins finally has a role he can sink his teeth into again, and Stellan Skarsgard scores as a doubtful scientist.  Natlie Portman doesn't have a whole lot to do, but she does it well; same with Kat Dennings, who provides some of the film's necessary comic relief.  Clark Gregg is back as smarmy Agent Coulson, and Idris Elba has a grand bit part as a loyal gatekeeper.  Also look for: Ray Stevenson as a Norse warrior; Rene Russo as Thor's mother; Colm Feore as a menacing Ice Giant king; and Jeremy Renner in a thoroughly bada** cameo.<br /><br />THOR definitely has its flaws, number one being its utter implausibility.  It's hard to bring the mystical wonder of comic books to the big screen; comic fans are much more willing to suspend disbelief than the average film-goer (though the popularity of such films as TWILIGHT and the TRANSFORMERS franchise says otherwise...).  Branagh doesn't try to convince us that all this is real; that tactic works great with heroes such as Batman, but when you're dealing with the Norse god of thunder, it's best to just shrug it off by having your hero declare: \\"Your ancestors called it magic; you call it science.  Where I come from, they're one and the same.\\"  Branagh goes for the implausible epic; he doesn't succeed (to keep audiences interested, the film has to clock in under 2 hours, and it's hard to do an epic is such a short time), but it's a noble effort, and makes for one of the more entertaining superhero movies of late.  Not quite as good as IRON MAN, say, but a few steps above IRON MAN 2 or THE GREEN LANTERN.  It's refreshing to see a comic book film that isn't an origin story, or doesn't try to rewrite \\"history.\\"  It's simply a straightforward film, featuring a solid cast, helmed by a reliable filmmaker.  If that's what you're in the mood for, it doesn't get much better than THOR.e from, they're one and the same.\\"  Branagh goes for the implausible epic; he doesn't succeed (to keep audiences interested, the film has to clock in under 2 hours, and it's hard to do an epic is such a short time), but it's a noble effort, and makes for one of the more entertaining superhero movies of late.
